  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_a216ce17-ee87-4550-bd3e-196fe1443090'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer2600258144791500900"><i>Twelve-thousand feet beneath the Atlantic Ocean . . .<br/>scientists are excavating the most extraordinary undersea discovery ever made. But is it the greatest archaeological find in history—or the most terrifying?</i><br/><br/>Former naval doctor Peter Crane is urgently summoned to a remote oil platform in the North Atlantic to help diagnose a bizarre medical condition spreading through the</span>
  <span id="freeText2600258144791500900" style="display:none"><i>Twelve-thousand feet beneath the Atlantic Ocean . . .<br />scientists are excavating the most extraordinary undersea discovery ever made. But is it the greatest archaeological find in history—or the most terrifying?</i><br /><br />Former naval doctor Peter Crane is urgently summoned to a remote oil platform in the North Atlantic to help diagnose a bizarre medical condition spreading through the rig. But when he arrives, Crane learns that the real trouble lies far below—on “Deep Storm,” a stunningly advanced science research facility built two miles beneath the surface on the ocean floor. The top secret structure has been designed for one purpose: to excavate a recently discovered undersea site that may hold the answers to a mystery steeped in centuries of myth and speculation.<br /><br />Sworn to secrecy, Dr. Crane descends to Deep Storm. A year earlier, he is told, routine drilling uncovered the remains of mankind’s most sophisticated ancient civilization: the legendary Atlantis. But now that the site is being excavated, a series of disturbing illnesses has begun to affect the operation. Scientists and technicians are experiencing a bizarre array of symptoms—from simple fatigue to violent psychotic episodes. As Crane is indoctrinated into the strange world of Deep Storm and commences his investigation, he begins to suspect that the covert facility conceals something more complicated than a medical mystery.The discovery of Atlantis might, in fact, be a cover for something far more sinister . . . and deadly.<br /><br />Like Lincoln Child’s spectacular bestsellers coauthored with Douglas Preston <i>(The Book of the Dead, Relic), Deep Storm</i> melds scientific detail and gripping adventure in a superbly imagined, chillingly real journey into unknown territory. Child is a master of suspense, and <i>Deep Storm</i> is his most ambitious novel to date.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2600258144791500900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<a href="/questions/1013051-why-is-it-that-people-think-they-re">Why is it that people think they&#39;re reviewing a book, when all they&#39;re doing is regurgitating the plot? Sometimes, word for word from the dust jacket. It&#39;s frustrating and often they spoil the plot. If you are going to review the book: read it, think about it, tell me what you think. Simple, no?</a>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/429069-this-book-is-listed-as-the-first-in-a">This book is listed as the first in a series featuring recurring protagonist Dr. Jeremy Logan, but he plot description doesn&#39;t mention this character.
Is Dr. Logan in this book, maybe just as a minor character? I&#39;m interested in reading this characters series, including the new novel, &quot;The Forgotten Room&quot;, and want to get caught up. 
Thanks!</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_429069'><a id="like_count_community_question_429069" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/429069?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">4 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F39026.Deep_Storm">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>

</li>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/14936611-bryan-hoogewerf"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_100x100-c7bfe730633c9983e27caaa66a8e244a.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/14936611-bryan-hoogewerf">Bryan Hoogewerf</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">The following is from an author's note promoting the latest Logan novel.<br/><br/>Considering that Jeremy Logan—an “enigmalogist” who studies phenomena beyond t<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">The following is from an author's note promoting the latest Logan novel.<br /><br />Considering that Jeremy Logan—an “enigmalogist” who studies phenomena beyond the bounds of regular science, and who proves (or disproves) things that most people would label occult or supernatural—is my main series character, his beginnings were very inauspicious. In fact, he first appeared in just a single chapter of my third novel, Deep Storm, as a researcher who stumbles upon a particularly important and unsettling fact that changes the course of the rest of the book. Logan didn’t even have a name at that point.<br /><br />As it turns out, my editor was much taken with the setting of that brief introduction, and with the character of Logan (however much a character can be delineated within the bounds of a single chapter), and urged me to send him on to do greater things. Coincidentally, this recommendation meshed with something that I had already been thinking about a great deal. Always a reader of ghost and horror stories, I was curious about the relatively forgotten mystery-thriller genre of the “ghost breaker” or “supernatural sleuth.” This was something that was very popular about a hundred years ago but, from my experience anyway, seemed less common today.<br /><br />And so, I set about turning Jeremy Logan, Yale professor of history, into someone who spent his off hours as a ghost detective; a researcher into the strange and inexplicable. There had been at least a faint hint of this fascination of mine as far back as Relic, my first collaboration with Doug Preston, but now I brought it fore and center. Even so, Logan made a rather gradual entrance onto the stage. In my novel following Deep Storm, Terminal Freeze, he has a lot more “screen time,” and yet is still not the obvious protagonist. It was not until the next book, The Third Gate, that he emerged fully fleshed out as the headline-catching enigmalogist of that story and the books that followed.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6980752159547682387">Another great Lincoln Child mystery (actually, this is the first of his solo projects I have read, but it has a similar feel to the ones he wrote with Preston). Sci-fi, twists and turns, shocking surprises, action sequences - from ancient documents to aliens - this book has it all! <br/><br/>The only reason only 4 stars is it did not keep moving through the center of the book. It seemed for quite some time there were a lot of scenes, but no advancement of the plot. But, it is just a minor complaint.<br/><br/>If you</span>
  <span id="freeText6980752159547682387" style="display:none">Another great Lincoln Child mystery (actually, this is the first of his solo projects I have read, but it has a similar feel to the ones he wrote with Preston). Sci-fi, twists and turns, shocking surprises, action sequences - from ancient documents to aliens - this book has it all! <br /><br />The only reason only 4 stars is it did not keep moving through the center of the book. It seemed for quite some time there were a lot of scenes, but no advancement of the plot. But, it is just a minor complaint.<br /><br />If you like all the things I mentioned in my first paragraph, strap on your diving suit and head to Deep Storm.</span>

<div id="review_319498320" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/319498320" />
    <a title="J213" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1609660-j213"><img alt="J213" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407209443p2/1609660.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/319498320?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J213" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J213" href="/user/show/1609660-j213">J213</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer319498320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer766848686827786738">Action movie and sci-fi thriller, but above all else, fluff. To me, Deep Storm is "one of those books." The kind you can you find on a rack in an airport, at a grocery store and in the $1 bin at a used book store. Fluff in the sense that it's pure escapism and temporary entertainment and I say "temporary" because once it's over, you've gained nothing about the world or about humanity. I realize that that might be an arrogant thing to say and I understand that there are always things to be learne</span>
  <span id="freeText766848686827786738" style="display:none">Action movie and sci-fi thriller, but above all else, fluff. To me, Deep Storm is "one of those books." The kind you can you find on a rack in an airport, at a grocery store and in the $1 bin at a used book store. Fluff in the sense that it's pure escapism and temporary entertainment and I say "temporary" because once it's over, you've gained nothing about the world or about humanity. I realize that that might be an arrogant thing to say and I understand that there are always things to be learned, but in a broad sense, you don't come away from stories like these feeling like you've been somewhere real or know the people you just read about, which of course is the essence of escapism. <br /><br />That said, there's nothing inherently wrong with that, it just isn't what I look for in literature. Deep Storm follows the beaten path and uses a blueprint for action thrillers that's been passed around the literary world as well as Hollywood for decades. As alarmingly predictable as it is, what kept me reading (and what keeps everyone reading, I'm guessing) was the premise and content of the story. The idea to Deep Storm is very intricate, smart and deeply scientific. Most chapters end on a cliffhanger and there are new developments and twists all along the way as well as plenty of other diversionary tactics to make you forget that not a single character has been or will be developed one bit.<br /><br />As theoretically possible as this underwater "Facility" is made to sound, it's still wildly fantastical and convenient for the author. Don't get me wrong, I definitely had fun reading it but it served a purpose to remind me why I read what I do.</span>
  <a data-text-id="766848686827786738" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_428083505" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/428083505" />
    <a title="Tanja Berg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5935491-tanja-berg"><img alt="Tanja Berg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558953813p2/5935491.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/428083505?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tanja Berg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tanja Berg" href="/user/show/5935491-tanja-berg">Tanja Berg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8126198-deep-storm">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5935491-tanja-berg?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5935491-tanja-berg?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer428083505" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10628320558137268799">I have shunned Lincoln Child, lifted my nose against this author when not as a duo with Douglas Preston. How unnecessary! This is the first book I've read by Child only and it was just as much fun. There is little to no precious insight into the human condition here, but I have to have variation. This was a a very entertaining science fiction thriller. <br/><br/>Dr. Crane is called out to the oil-drilling platform of Deep Storm because the crew is suffering from various ailments. Not on the platform itsel</span>
  <span id="freeText10628320558137268799" style="display:none">I have shunned Lincoln Child, lifted my nose against this author when not as a duo with Douglas Preston. How unnecessary! This is the first book I've read by Child only and it was just as much fun. There is little to no precious insight into the human condition here, but I have to have variation. This was a a very entertaining science fiction thriller. <br /><br />Dr. Crane is called out to the oil-drilling platform of Deep Storm because the crew is suffering from various ailments. Not on the platform itself, but in the technological facility below. He is told Atlantis has been found, but isn't cleared for further details. The hundreds of people who have fallen ill all have a variation of symptoms - nausea, numbness, stroke symptoms and psychotic episodes. Of course events unfold from bad to worse at an ever-increasing pace. It's what you expect from this sort of book, particularly when both the military and scientists are involved. Brute power means brute force, sooner or later.<br /><br />I kept second-guessing what was really going on all through the book. All the answers did not become clear until the epilogue. Which is the way I like it. I really hate it when my theories on page 10 or so turn out to be right at the end. This book delivered according to expectations, revealing answers only little by little.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10628320558137268799" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1548119221" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1548119221" />
    <a title="Jaksen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7233842-jaksen"><img alt="Jaksen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455934399p2/7233842.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1548119221?book_show_action=true">Feb 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaksen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaksen" href="/user/show/7233842-jaksen">Jaksen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1548119221" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11191960540107380383">A good enough adventure story, set at the bottom of the Atlantic where the ruins of Atlantis - maybe - have been found. A lot of technical detail and research went into this novel, and though not my favorite by Mr. Child, I would not hesitate to read further in this series by him. <br/><br/>(In fact, I'd already read 'The Forgotten Room,' No. 4 in the series and absolutely loved it.)<br/><br/>Where it bogs down is in all the characters rushing around from the middle to the end. (I call this the 'Lost Effect,' named</span>
  <span id="freeText11191960540107380383" style="display:none">A good enough adventure story, set at the bottom of the Atlantic where the ruins of Atlantis - maybe - have been found. A lot of technical detail and research went into this novel, and though not my favorite by Mr. Child, I would not hesitate to read further in this series by him. <br /><br />(In fact, I'd already read 'The Forgotten Room,' No. 4 in the series and absolutely loved it.)<br /><br />Where it bogs down is in all the characters rushing around from the middle to the end. (I call this the 'Lost Effect,' named for one of my (former) fav. television shows which got bogged down in its final year by having groups of random characters wandering around aimlessly.) In 'Deep Storm' new characters are introduced rather late and too many of the regulars are stereotypical, IMO. This includes the commander who is overbearing and super-uber-military, the kind who forges ahead with a 'damned-be-the-consequences' attitude. (Yes, a stereotype often exists because of the truth behind it, but I get tired of them. Maybe I read too much.)<br /><br />People get hit on the head; things are blown up; there are mysterious happenings including a rash of strange ailments. There's also an 'alien race' angle and did they visit Earth centuries ago and why. My fav. part of the book is when Jeremy Logan, a renowned historian/researcher visits an old monastery in order to research incidents occurring in the North Atlantic over the last 600 years. <br /><br />Anyhow, the perfect tale for a Tom Clancy afficionado, with a deep underwater appeal. But just three stars, my rating for so-so, not bad, yet not outstanding. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11191960540107380383" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1958420578" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4755220138657393470">Misleading to call this a part of the Jeremy Logan series<br/><br/>Right before this book I read FULL WOLF MOON, the 5th book in the Jeremy Logan series. I liked it so much that I downloaded the other four books in the series, with DEEP STORM showing as the first. Well, out of this whole lengthy book, Jeremy Logan is mentioned on one, maybe two, pages.<br/><br/>That clarified, I really did enjoy this offering from author Lincoln Child.<br/><br/>Retired Naval Dr. Peter Crane is summoned to the Storm King oil platform forty mi</span>
  <span id="freeText4755220138657393470" style="display:none">Misleading to call this a part of the Jeremy Logan series<br /><br />Right before this book I read FULL WOLF MOON, the 5th book in the Jeremy Logan series. I liked it so much that I downloaded the other four books in the series, with DEEP STORM showing as the first. Well, out of this whole lengthy book, Jeremy Logan is mentioned on one, maybe two, pages.<br /><br />That clarified, I really did enjoy this offering from author Lincoln Child.<br /><br />Retired Naval Dr. Peter Crane is summoned to the Storm King oil platform forty miles offshore from Greenland. He finds that his particular background is perfect for a Top Secret operation going on on the sea floor under Storm King. The military has spared no expense in preparing an underwater research/excavation facility in that location. And Dr. Crane had to sign a multitude of non-disclosure statements to even descend to the facility, which the crew has named DEEP STORM.<br /><br />There was a lot of science thrown at the reader throughout the book but the author did a great job of breaking it down into small parts and explaining it. Dr. Crane was the protagonist of the story and he found his role in the project confusing at times (as did I) but that evened out probably halfway through the book. <br /><br />The story was exciting and (semi) believable but it did kind of bog down in the middle of the book.<br /><br />I like underwater stories, adventure stories, thriller stories, and stories of the Arctic, Antarctic, Greenland or Iceland so this book fit these categories very well. I'm going to read TERMINAL FREEZE next, the second book in the Jeremy Logan series, and hopefully Logan will make more of an appearance.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4755220138657393470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5479921467266602410" style="display:none">The following may well explain what I think about this book by Lincoln Child; I read it in four days! <br />I am normally a slow reader, and I mix reading with writing my own novels. Deep Storm took over. In places this book challenges one’s thinking because a certain amount of computer science crops up in the plot. Don’t let that put you off reading this book, after all it is a techno thriller and a certain amount of working things out is to be expected. <br />The novel is well paced, although possibly a little slow to get going, but once the plot begins to be hinted at it does grip the mind and becomes a page-turner. <br />If you like a story that involves mystery, a smattering of history, action, threat and some science thrown in, then this is the book for you.</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer103243640" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5064778733336219284">Lincoln Child blew me away with this one. I'm a huge, huge fan, and I've always loved his solo-novels, but this one has to be my favorite of his.<br/><br/>This book is pretty sneaky. It likes to smirk a bit when it leads you astray, and then laugh in your face when you find out the plot has twisted. I had my theories, from page one, and said theories changed, morphed, were pulled apart, thrown away, re-born, etc... and I still got surprised in the end.<br/><br/>He wastes no time, every page is just packed with awes</span>
  <span id="freeText5064778733336219284" style="display:none">Lincoln Child blew me away with this one. I'm a huge, huge fan, and I've always loved his solo-novels, but this one has to be my favorite of his.<br /><br />This book is pretty sneaky. It likes to smirk a bit when it leads you astray, and then laugh in your face when you find out the plot has twisted. I had my theories, from page one, and said theories changed, morphed, were pulled apart, thrown away, re-born, etc... and I still got surprised in the end.<br /><br />He wastes no time, every page is just packed with awesome. From the first to the last, you get pretty hooked.<br /><br />Everybody who loves science, military operations, sci-fi, mystery, thriller, oceanic expeditions, etc... should read this.<br /><br />Brava.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2348023720" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2348023720" />
    <a title="audrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1962180-audrey"><img alt="audrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564620395p2/1962180.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2348023720?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="audrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="audrey" href="/user/show/1962180-audrey">audrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1962180-audrey?shelf=weird-feelings-about-architecture">weird-feelings-about-architecture</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1962180-audrey?shelf=fear-of-the-feminine">fear-of-the-feminine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1962180-audrey?shelf=haunted-boat-story">haunted-boat-story</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1962180-audrey?shelf=brandy-you-re-a-fine-girl">brandy-you-re-a-fine-girl</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2348023720&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2348023720" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11464100487365919216"><blockquote>Crane frowned. "It's your call to make, of course. But seventy-two hours ago, this 'simple mood disorder' took a hostage, then jammed a screwdriver into his own throat."<br/><br/>Corbett took a sip of his latte. "Waite clearly has some issues to grapple with."</blockquote><br/><br/>Deep Storm is a really really top-secret government project taking place two miles under the surface of the ocean that the awesome Dr. Peter Crane is called aboard to troubleshoot. See, it turns out that it's making people on the deepwater station in</span>
  <span id="freeText11464100487365919216" style="display:none"><blockquote>Crane frowned. "It's your call to make, of course. But seventy-two hours ago, this 'simple mood disorder' took a hostage, then jammed a screwdriver into his own throat."<br /><br />Corbett took a sip of his latte. "Waite clearly has some issues to grapple with."</blockquote><br /><br />Deep Storm is a really really top-secret government project taking place two miles under the surface of the ocean that the awesome Dr. Peter Crane is called aboard to troubleshoot. See, it turns out that it's making people on the deepwater station insane to be doing all that top secret research, and then they wander round and take women hostage with screwdrivers. But more importantly, they don't keep at their top secret research and that right there friends, that's the very definition of a problem. <br /><br />Oh who cares if the lady with the screwdriver in her neck was harmed! SCIENCE IS IN DANGER!<br /><br />Dr Awesome Crane, who takes pains to tell us multiple times about how he was debating between which of two prestigious research positions to take when he was called to storm and storm deep, runs about the research station attempting to troubleshoot all the crazy people while not having enough clearance to know about the top secret things they're doing. He'd love to puzzle it out with the doctor who's been on-board roughly nine times longer than him but she's a woman and she doesn't immediately drop and worship his huge throbbing brain so he decides to "put her in her place". Too bad she turns out to be as smart as he is. OOH SNAP.<br /><br />You know what this means, right?<br /><br />That woman must be one of the villains! I mean, there are five main characters in the book, only one of whom is female, this pesky know-it-all-doctoring-woman, so you know, the odds are really good she's a villain. How do we know this? Because she's as smart as the hero and yet does not fly at him with her legs spread. VILLAINY! VILLAINY! Gosh-darn it! Girl police! Get her!<br /><br />There is a supporting female character -- and I say "a" here deliberately because in a cast of hundreds of walk-on dudes on a research station, there is one woman with a speaking part* and verily she is smart too and she is a computer geek and she figures out the steganography subplot long before anyone else, but then, during the crucial escape sequence, she loses her nerve, freaks out, cries and -- wait for it -- has to have Dr Awesome carry her bodily to safety while she blubbers round his shoulders. <br /><br />Oh he totally wants to date her by the end of the book too, in possibly the creepiest way possible. Dr Awesome simple decides that whatever his future holds, IT WILL HOLD THAT WOMAN. And then she smiles simperingly back at him. <br /><br />Now, you're going to have to all excuse me if I sound cynical or fed up or bitter and hating, but there's just so many stories a person can read where the script goes, AND THE WOMEN WHO ARE SMART, THEY ARE EITHER EVIL OR THEY CRY. LIKE, A LOT. before you realize that's the basic plot of way too many books, telling women that if they're smart, their place in any narrative is to either be evil, or crying and in need of rescue. <br /><br />What does that tell women about their own stories? What does it tell them about their place in it? Why can't we be heroes that don't have to date the dude who's in love with his own brain? <br /><br />That bit, I admit, sent the book skywards. There was zero and I do mean ZERO chemistry between Dr Awesome and the steganography geek, and no hints of romance until at the end, after he's carried her to safety, he turns around and goes YUP, SHE'LL DO. I'LL TAKE HER TO GO. <br /><br />Oh who doesn't want to be chosen to mate with Dr. Awesome and his big head? <br /><br /><br /><br /><br />*No, the hostage chick just got to scream. And cry.**<br />**As did the woman climbing onto the escape pod, only she cried and gave in to the big bad villains, necessitating Dr. Awesome and another dude to save her.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="hardcover 9789512074495" href="/book/show/6063189-uusi-atlantis">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75684652-venla?shelf=thrillers">thrillers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75684652-venla?shelf=2019">2019</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2831086475" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3249953637399616785">Just a Lincoln Child fan here who is absolutely in love with this series already! Deep Storm (Uusi Atlantis in Finnish) is the first book in the Jeremy Logan series. I've read the whole Pendergast series before tackling this one which is why had really high expectations and luckily, my expectations were met and I enjoyed reading this.<br/><br/>Lincoln Child is known for having many scientific and historical details in his books and Deep Storm was actually build on top of many scientific discoveries, a hin</span>
  <span id="freeText3249953637399616785" style="display:none">Just a Lincoln Child fan here who is absolutely in love with this series already! Deep Storm (Uusi Atlantis in Finnish) is the first book in the Jeremy Logan series. I've read the whole Pendergast series before tackling this one which is why had really high expectations and luckily, my expectations were met and I enjoyed reading this.<br /><br />Lincoln Child is known for having many scientific and historical details in his books and Deep Storm was actually build on top of many scientific discoveries, a hint of history and a significant amount of scifi vibes. What makes this book so great is the way the whole story is build piece by piece. Every little detail is linked together somehow and the depiction of the events is so realistic that you get lost in the settings. <br /><br />In Deep Storm, doctor Peter Crane is summoned to a remote oil platform to take part in a top-secret operation that might not seem like anything interesting at first glance. Little do Crane know what kind of mess is waiting for him under the water surface. <br /><br />The book just pulled me along and I read it in one sitting. I got pretty carried away because the story got more and more interesting as more facts were revealed and the big picture started to reveal itself. I already have the next book and I'm way too excited about it already.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3249953637399616785" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16449963612533295235">I always enjoy this author rather he writes alone or with his co-author Douglas Preston. There was a lot of science in the book...however Lincoln Child did an excellent job of explaining it and giving it to us a little at a time. Dr. Crane was our protagonist. I felt rather sorry for him. I think that he found his role in the project confusing at times...and he didn't always have the final say which was strange for a man that was supposed to be in charge of something this big. The story remained</span>
  <span id="freeText16449963612533295235" style="display:none">I always enjoy this author rather he writes alone or with his co-author Douglas Preston. There was a lot of science in the book...however Lincoln Child did an excellent job of explaining it and giving it to us a little at a time. Dr. Crane was our protagonist. I felt rather sorry for him. I think that he found his role in the project confusing at times...and he didn't always have the final say which was strange for a man that was supposed to be in charge of something this big. The story remained exciting until the very end. The reader just has to find out what this project was actually about and it really was more mystery than science fiction. There are numerous things at play in the book producing a steady feeling of suspense and paranoia. Anyone that has read the books that Lincoln Child has written with Douglas Preston will like this offering.</span>

  <span id="freeText10283830540577660066" style="display:none">This is the first Lincoln Child book that I have read and I was not disappointed. I enjoyed the science fiction / thriller theme. <div id="review_228721520" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/228721520" />
    <a title="hayden" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2995566-hayden"><img alt="hayden" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598996671p2/2995566.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/228721520?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="hayden" class="user" itemprop="url" name="hayden" href="/user/show/2995566-hayden">hayden</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307946720" href="/book/show/10426993-deep-storm">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of the genre, of which I am not</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to hayden by: </span>
      mommy dearest

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=dont-get-why-its-so-appealing">dont-get-why-its-so-appealing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=dont-give-a-hoot">dont-give-a-hoot</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=dreadfully-boring">dreadfully-boring</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=ending-rocked">ending-rocked</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=deathly-long-descriptions">deathly-long-descriptions</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=ending-didnt-make-sense">ending-didnt-make-sense</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=series-dropped">series-dropped</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=thank-god-it-s-a-stand-alone">thank-god-it-s-a-stand-alone</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer228721520" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7638023386444191217">This was another hand-me-down airport novel donated from the lovely mother. Here's the deal with Deep Storm: while reading it, I felt super conflicted. It was written really well, the story was interesting enough, and the plot moved at a whiplash-breakneck-pace, yet I <i>couldn't have wanted to put it down more.</i> Seriously. My extreme need to drop this book where I stood (or, more accurately, sat) was immeasurably high, for an inexplicable reason. But I did.<br/><br/>In Deep Storm, there was a lot of submarin</span>
  <span id="freeText7638023386444191217" style="display:none">This was another hand-me-down airport novel donated from the lovely mother. Here's the deal with Deep Storm: while reading it, I felt super conflicted. It was written really well, the story was interesting enough, and the plot moved at a whiplash-breakneck-pace, yet I <i>couldn't have wanted to put it down more.</i> Seriously. My extreme need to drop this book where I stood (or, more accurately, sat) was immeasurably high, for an inexplicable reason. But I did.<br><br>In Deep Storm, there was a lot of submarine and technological terminology, and I held onto or understood about 0.001% of it. Things like "hull" and "electromagnetic link" and "degausser" . . . yeesh. Maybe I'm just retarded or I didn't pay enough attention in 8th grade history class.<br><br>I've never been one that fancied airport novels. In fact, my whole life I've tried to stay away from them. Seeing <b>James Patterson</b> in big, bold letters on a book cover makes me cringe (and not just for the rights issues). I cringe away from them because they're all so . . . predictable. Which is one thing this book was not. I was totally not expecting the ending this book had.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380362093i/713195.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>It just jumped out at me, and honestly, it kind of made the entire book pointless. When the characters don't get anywhere in a book, it pisses me off <i>majorly.</i> That is my biggest pet peeve in literature: books that go nowhere, which is why sitcoms bug me so much. They always start in the same place and end in the same place.<br><br>Lincoln Child doesn't seem like a guy I'm going to buy on a whim at an airport the next time my flight is delayed. In fact, after this reading experience, I think I'm just going to drop all related authors. These books do nothing for me. Nothing!<br><br>Clive Cussler said this book was "harrowing and brilliantly conceived." No wonder I hated <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5867840.Medusa__NUMA_Files___8_" title="Medusa (NUMA Files, #8) by Clive Cussler" rel="nofollow noopener">Medusa</a> so much if you think this book is any of those things you said about it.<br><br>Another thing about this book that bugged me is that none of the characters really did anything for me. None of them were any different from the next. They were all scientist/marine biologist carbon copies, and when the end came, I didn't care whether they all lived or died. I actually couldn't have cared less.<br><br>I actually kind of liked the book for a few chapters, but after those initial few I began to think, "By god, when is this thing going to end?" And that's a correct assumption to make. The thing is 370 pages with the smallest print imaginable. I think Child could've taken some things out and made it about 300, at the most. In the middle, I got really bored, and I skipped over the rest until I got to the end. Lincoln Child drew a few yawns out of me before I finally "finished" the book.<br><br>Deep Storm is nothing special in the world of literary gems, besides the jaw-dropping ending and the fairly good writing. It's definitely nothing you need to spend $14.95 (or, as my mom paid for it half off, $7.48) on.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7638023386444191217" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer10378125897369935662">There are going to be some obvious comparisons between this book and Michael Crichton's <i>Sphere.</i> Well, I loved both, each for their own story. While both deal with a mix of military & scientists deep underwater investigating a mysterious artifact, with tons of plot twists, each tale is presented differently. <br/><br/>Ultimately, Child's novel is less personal and more epic. There are quite a number of characters and sub-plots, textbooks worth of scientific information being thrown at you during the increa</span>
  <span id="freeText10378125897369935662" style="display:none">There are going to be some obvious comparisons between this book and Michael Crichton's <i>Sphere.</i> Well, I loved both, each for their own story. While both deal with a mix of military &amp; scientists deep underwater investigating a mysterious artifact, with tons of plot twists, each tale is presented differently. <br /><br />Ultimately, Child's novel is less personal and more epic. There are quite a number of characters and sub-plots, textbooks worth of scientific information being thrown at you during the increasingly tense novel. By the time you've finished <i>Deep Storm,</i> you'll realize it has very little in common with Crichton's novel other than what would amount to a paragraph description.<br /><br />Reads like any other Preston-Child book of awesomeness, with that Crichton/Cussler feel. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10307803414395725124">This book is extremely well written, and is a horrific mystery with all the grotesque details. The book itself keeps you on the edge of your seat, although a bit slow in the very beginning. The build up of the main character, Dr. Crane, is interesting to say the least, as it slowly allows details about him, but never fully interpreting them for the reader, which allows for more imagination to take place. Also, you will likely need a dictionary with you every time you read, as there is tons of li</span>
  <span id="freeText10307803414395725124" style="display:none">This book is extremely well written, and is a horrific mystery with all the grotesque details. The book itself keeps you on the edge of your seat, although a bit slow in the very beginning. The build up of the main character, Dr. Crane, is interesting to say the least, as it slowly allows details about him, but never fully interpreting them for the reader, which allows for more imagination to take place. Also, you will likely need a dictionary with you every time you read, as there is tons of lingo and a rather broad use of vocabulary within this text. This author clearly did their research on everything within this piece of literature, and it has made the story all the more realistic and frightening. But what do they find underneath earth's crust? You'll just have to read to find out.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18028988685018725682">I got about 150 pages into this, and realized that I just didn't care anymore! I think the concept of this book is interesting, but Child's writing is frustrating. He seems to want to "wow" us with his knowledge of technical jargon in the medical, military, AND computer fields. Throw in some vocabulary about oil platforms and submarines, and you've basically got the book. The protagonist is a flat character, one you never really care about because he's got no personality.<br/>Overall, disappointing, </span>
  <span id="freeText18028988685018725682" style="display:none">I got about 150 pages into this, and realized that I just didn't care anymore! I think the concept of this book is interesting, but Child's writing is frustrating. He seems to want to "wow" us with his knowledge of technical jargon in the medical, military, AND computer fields. Throw in some vocabulary about oil platforms and submarines, and you've basically got the book. The protagonist is a flat character, one you never really care about because he's got no personality.<br />Overall, disappointing, unless you enjoy a little science fiction mixed with military secrets and very little relationship development.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2909866548" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17643291024387196261">This was an interesting story line, definitely unique. A bit of sci-fi, a bit of suspense. A "lotta" details. This is what I'd consider a guy's read, heavy on detail (did I mention there's a lot of details) and light on relationships and/or character development.<br/><br/>But it was a fun read, and I'm sure I'll be visiting my Lincoln Child again.<br/><br/>Note: Perhaps minor language, no other R rated portions.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_429953050" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/429953050" />
    <a title="Mangzilla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4091384-mangzilla"><img alt="Mangzilla" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/429953050?book_show_action=true">Oct 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mangzilla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mangzilla" href="/user/show/4091384-mangzilla">Mangzilla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4091384-mangzilla?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4091384-mangzilla?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer429953050&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer429953050" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17220086686529318751">Though an easy read, I found the scientific/technical flaws very upsetting. <br/><br/>[Spoilers below]<br/>The sentinels are supposedly emitting a light with a signal that came from the "star" "Cygnus Major". Cygnus is a constellation, there is no Cygnus Major. There's only a "Major" when there is also a "Minor", as in Ursa Major/Minor, or Canis Major/Minor. Also, there's no real use made of this "fact", it's just supposed to indicate their alien origin.<br/><br/>I'm a computer programmer, with computer security experti</span>
  <span id="freeText17220086686529318751" style="display:none">Though an easy read, I found the scientific/technical flaws very upsetting. <br /><br />[Spoilers below]<br />The sentinels are supposedly emitting a light with a signal that came from the "star" "Cygnus Major". Cygnus is a constellation, there is no Cygnus Major. There's only a "Major" when there is also a "Minor", as in Ursa Major/Minor, or Canis Major/Minor. Also, there's no real use made of this "fact", it's just supposed to indicate their alien origin.<br /><br />I'm a computer programmer, with computer security expertise. I appreciated his use of steganography ( hiding a secret message within another message, a picture).<br /><br />Supposedly the computer he was on was "secured" so that he couldn't upload a program. It could read and write CDs, but somehow he wasn't supposed to be able to execute a program from an external source. <br />He is supposed to get around these security measures by entering the short program mentioned above, and then _compiling_ it. Duh! You don't put a compiler on a restricted system.<br /><br />The short computer program that he included was totally bogus. With "void main(void)" to start, it was obviously supposed to be written in the "C" programming language, but the curly braces "{}", and semicolons were missing. The shift expressions were incorrect, and he was using "return" in some weird illogical way.<br /><br />The program was supposed to replace a couple of the least-significant bits in each pixel with bits extracted from the secret message. This would be seen as "noise" in the picture and ignored. Well, that would have worked if the picture file used raw bytes for pixels, in a .bmp files, say. But Mr. Child used a .jpg file. It's much more common, but it just doesn't work that way. It is compressed. A tiny change in the bytes would, almost certainly, drastically change the picture.<br /><br />I also didn't think he understood the realities of deep pressure. The whole sea-bottom facility was unrealistic. At one point a pinhole sized leak cuts off a man's fingers, later the hero climbs a ladder though a gushing breach while a woman clings to his back. <br /><br />The so-called "pressure spokes" don't seem to obey any physics. What good is it to let the outside pressure into the middle of the facility?<br /><br />I re-read this book because I could not remember the ending. Now I know that the ending is rather anti-climactic. That's probably why I forgot it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17220086686529318751" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2987749830" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2987749830" />
    <a title="❀ Crystal ✿ -  PEACE ☮ LOVE ♥ BOOKS " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10734393-crystal---peace-love-books"><img alt="❀ Crystal ✿ -  PEACE ☮ LOVE ♥ BOOKS " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1396916584p2/10734393.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2987749830?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="❀ Crystal ✿ -  PEACE ☮ LOVE ♥ BOOKS " class="user" itemprop="url" name="❀ Crystal ✿ -  PEACE ☮ LOVE ♥ BOOKS " href="/user/show/10734393-crystal---peace-love-books">❀ Crystal ✿ -  PEACE ☮ LOVE ♥ BOOKS </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10734393-crystal---peace-love-books?shelf=2019-read">2019-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2987749830" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3800283259520745619">To be honest I really wasn't a fan of this book. Granted I will admit I listened to the audio version and this could be why my reaction is less than stellar. The narration was amazing but there were not chapters mentioned and so scenes would shift between characters often and it was hard to follow along. Also I found myself being often distracted, this wasn't the most engaging read. I was let down when the mystery turned out to not be Atlantis but rather some alien technology left in the bottom </span>
  <span id="freeText3800283259520745619" style="display:none">To be honest I really wasn't a fan of this book. Granted I will admit I listened to the audio version and this could be why my reaction is less than stellar. The narration was amazing but there were not chapters mentioned and so scenes would shift between characters often and it was hard to follow along. Also I found myself being often distracted, this wasn't the most engaging read. I was let down when the mystery turned out to not be Atlantis but rather some alien technology left in the bottom of our ocean to warn other races to stay away from our planet. Of course humans as we are can't leave things alone, we have to control it, understand it and if possible turn it into a weapon smh. There are two black holes buried under the sea and a corrupted military general or captain just won't give up the 'find of the century,' weapons that could potentially destroy more than just our planet. I will say the thought of an active alien weapons storage beneath the sea is a bit disheartening. But given the context of the story, it's beyond even our simplest understanding so as long as we leave it be we just have to hope these aliens don't come back during our lifetime for their weapons. <br />The story overall was OK, not what I was expecting and it kind of dragged. Some of the characters were interesting, like Dr. Ping who had some closet computer skills that came in handy. I did not enjoy the military aspect, no matter real life or fiction, there is always someone who goes bad and whether they argue they are acting in their countries best interest, it's often selfish in nature such as the case with General Corolis. Peter Crane wasn't a phenomenal MC personally and while he was smart and kept the story moving, I was only glad someone was smart enough to piece together the puzzle of the sentinels in a research facility filled with brilliant scientists.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3800283259520745619" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_80949958" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/80949958" />
    <a title="kingshearte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3031302-kingshearte"><img alt="kingshearte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573912877p2/3031302.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/80949958?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="kingshearte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="kingshearte" href="/user/show/3031302-kingshearte">kingshearte</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3031302-kingshearte?shelf=fluff">fluff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3031302-kingshearte?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3031302-kingshearte?shelf=2009">2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer80949958" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13060626709864308703"><i>Former naval doctor Peter Crane is urgently summoned to a remote oil platform in the North Atlantic to help diagnose a bizarre medical condition spreading through the rig. But when he arrives, Crane learns that the real trouble lies far below -- on "Deep Storm," a stunningly advanced science research facility built two miles beneath the surface on the ocean floor. The top-secret structure has been designed for one purpose: to excavate a recently discovered undersea site that may hold the answers</i></span>
  <span id="freeText13060626709864308703" style="display:none"><i>Former naval doctor Peter Crane is urgently summoned to a remote oil platform in the North Atlantic to help diagnose a bizarre medical condition spreading through the rig. But when he arrives, Crane learns that the real trouble lies far below -- on "Deep Storm," a stunningly advanced science research facility built two miles beneath the surface on the ocean floor. The top-secret structure has been designed for one purpose: to excavate a recently discovered undersea site that may hold the answers to a mystery steeped in centuries of myth and speculation.<br /><br />Sworn to secrecy, Dr. Crane descends to Deep Storm. A year earlier, he is told, routine drilling uncovered the remains of mankind's most sophisticated civilization: the legendary Atlantis. But now that the site is being excavated, a series of disturbing illnesses has begun to affect the operation. Scientists and technicians are experiencing a bizarre array of symptoms -- from simple fatigue to violent psychotic episodes. As Crane is indoctrinated into the strange world of Deep Storm and commences his investigation, he begins to suspect that the covert facility conceals something more complicated than a medical mystery. The discovery of Atlantis might, in fact, be a cover for something far more sinister... and deadly.</i><br /><br />This book was what it was. Which is basically the male equivalent of chick-lit. You've got your action, your excessive unnecessary detail that might as well have a sign pointing to it reading, "My grade school English teacher told me that good writing is descriptive, so here are some descriptive details," your people all referred to by last name (which was particularly funny immediately after one character assured another that they were all on a first-name basis here), and you've got your military presence. As such things go, though, this one wasn't bad. It didn't have the author writing himself in to pass off the key piece of information because he couldn't think of any other way for the hero to get it, so that sets Child up at least a notch from Clive Cussler.<br /><br />I think it was a bit too long, though. Until Crane starts to figure out what's going on, you just feel like you're floundering, and while Child certainly piles on the gruesome deaths in an effort to keep things interesting, and make sure that he really hammers home that SOMETHING BAD IS HAPPENING, really, it got dull. If a chapter opened with an introduction to a new character unrelated to any of the established plot lines, you pretty much knew he was going to die a horrible death within the next few pages. I picked out the mole a little bit before the reveal, but looking back, there were clues, so that's pretty good. His red herring was OK, but a little too obvious to ever really suspect him. The developing romance was also pretty predictable.<br /><br />I did like, though, the way he left it at the end. Everything pretty much tied up, and then bam, one more revelation that leaves things up in the air just a little bit. Not even in a sequel kind of way, since a sequel would pretty much force Child into pure sci-fi territory, but in a make-the-book-stay-with-you-just-for-a-bit kind of way. Which is kind of an accomplishment for a book of this nature, since there really isn't all that much to it.<br /><br />Anyway, it was a decent read. Fulfilled its purpose. Oh, and unlike most chick-lit-for-dudes I've read, this one actually had a few female characters who were actually developed, and existed for more than just a lust interest for the hero. So yay for that, too.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13060626709864308703" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7564237494147572282">It was interesting and I liked how it was written, but it just didn't blow me away.<br/>I appreciated, that the author tried to give the characters interests and traits, but except for Asher, I never cared for anyone longterm.<br/>Some twists were good, others were not that exciting. <br/>There were some bits were the book defied my expectations, but it also was a lot like I imagine every other story like this to be.<br/>In generell, I had fun, but I doubt this will leave any kind of lasting impression. However, I </span>
  <span id="freeText7564237494147572282" style="display:none">It was interesting and I liked how it was written, but it just didn't blow me away.<br />I appreciated, that the author tried to give the characters interests and traits, but except for Asher, I never cared for anyone longterm.<br />Some twists were good, others were not that exciting. <br />There were some bits were the book defied my expectations, but it also was a lot like I imagine every other story like this to be.<br />In generell, I had fun, but I doubt this will leave any kind of lasting impression. However, I don't think it was written with that intent anyhow. It's designed to be readable for the masses, with no elements that would throw many people off. If you like this kind of research mystery / thriller, I guess you would like this one as well. For me, there was nothing special about it, but it was still easy to get through and enjoy.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('uZE84EeKceF/faxZQOlPbzYoj9PLoD3UQUNWiWsaXPEfi+F91eNk9HddKYYMv2gueyYB+QnVhBAMUZUSIms8Zw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('zB6HIXJ+0fvUrxPOPKUNJc/CMnJPeeqUBAxE0o24+4VqBFq84BfE7tyPlhFw8ypkgsy8WI0MU1BJHodJxMmbEw==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/11091.Lincoln_Child">About Lincoln Child</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/11091.Lincoln_Child"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1437511558p3/11091.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10803693969632327253">Lincoln Child was born in Westport, Connecticut, which he still calls his hometown (despite the fact that he left the place before he reached his first birthday and now only goes back for weekends).<br/><br/>Lincoln seemed to have acquired an interest in writing as early as second grade, when he wrote a short story entitled Bumble the Elephant (now believed by scholars to be lost). Along with two dozen shor</span>
  <span id="freeText10803693969632327253" style="display:none">Lincoln Child was born in Westport, Connecticut, which he still calls his hometown (despite the fact that he left the place before he reached his first birthday and now only goes back for weekends).<br /><br />Lincoln seemed to have acquired an interest in writing as early as second grade, when he wrote a short story entitled Bumble the Elephant (now believed by scholars to be lost). Along with two dozen short stories composed during his youth, he wrote a science-fiction novel in tenth grade called Second Son of Daedalus and a shamelessly Tolkeinesque fantasy in twelfth grade titled The Darkness to the North (left unfinished at 400 manuscript pages). Both are exquisitely embarrassing to read today and are kept under lock and key by the author.<br /><br />After a childhood that is of interest only to himself, Lincoln graduated from Carleton College (huh?) in Northfield, Minnesota, majoring in English. Discovering a fascination for words, and their habit of turning up in so many books, he made his way to New York in the summer of 1979, intent on finding a job in publishing. He was lucky enough to secure a position as editorial assistant at St. Martin's Press.<br /><br />Over the next several years, he clawed his way up the editorial hierarchy, moving to assistant editor to associate editor before becoming a full editor in 1984. While at St. Martin's, he was associated with the work of many authors, including that of James Herriot and M. M. Kaye. He edited well over a hundred books--with titles as diverse as The Notation of Western Music and Hitler's Rocket Sites--but focused primarily on American and English popular fiction.<br /><br />While at St. Martin's, Lincoln assembled several collections of ghost and horror stories, beginning with the hardcover collections Dark Company (1984) and Dark Banquet (1985). Later, when he founded the company's mass-market horror division, he edited three more collections of ghost stories, Tales of the Dark 1-3.<br /><br />In 1987, Lincoln left trade publishing to work at MetLife. In a rather sudden transition, he went from editing manuscripts, speaking at sales conferences, and wining/dining agents to doing highly technical programming and systems analysis. Though the switch might seem bizarre, Lincoln was a propeller-head from a very early age, and his extensive programming experience dates back to high school, when he worked with DEC minis and the now-prehistoric IBM 1620, so antique it actually had an electric typewriter mounted into its front panel. Away from the world of publishing, Lincoln's own nascent interests in writing returned. While at MetLife, Relic was published, and within a few years Lincoln had left the company to write full time. He now lives in New Jersey (under protest--just kidding) with his wife and daughter.<br /><br />A dilettante by natural inclination, Lincoln's interests include: pre-1950s literature and poetry; post-1950s popular fiction; playing the piano, various MIDI instruments, and the 5-string banjo; English and American history; motorcycles; architecture; classical music, early jazz, blues, and R&amp;B; exotic parrots; esoteric programming languages; mountain hiking; bow ties; Italian suits; fedoras; archaeology; and multiplayer deathmatching.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10803693969632327253" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
